Data Base Editor

User's Guide

This users guide corresponds to level 9R1 of DBE. Features described in this document, while being implemented as described herein, are subject to change or deletion at any time.

This manual provides instruction and reference to users wishing to learn about the database editor or use its "canned" functions. Those users interested in exercising the "progammability" of the database editor should consult the "Database Editor Programmers Reference Manual".

Consult Appendix B for a complete summary of all DBE changes made to date.

Please note that DBE is not an official software product of UNISYS Corporation and therefore falls into none of the standard software support categories.

Problem reports may be submitted to:

Albany, NY 12205                 Albany, NY 12243

Such reported problems will be handled on an "as time allows" basis.

This document is intended to provide the reader with all information necessary to operate the DBE processor.

There is an additional DBE document available - the 'Database Editor Technical Guide', providing a technical overview of the construction, generation and maintenance of the processor itself. This document will likely be of interest only to the technician whose job it is to maintain DBE.

| Chapter 1 | Functions of the |

| INTRODUCTION | Database Editor |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1. An Introduction to the Database Editor

1.1. Functions of the Database Editor

This chapter is intended to serve as an introduction to the database editor [DBE] utility. DBE provides a powerful interactive facility for use by the DMS/1100 support technician and database administrator.

By supplying DBE with an absolute schema, the DBE user may 'browse' through a DMS/1100 database using commands similar to the 1100 series text editor (@ED); the various tables and data structures within an absolute schema or subschema may be examined using the same commands that allow the inspection of database pages. A series of generalized I/O commands allow external files such as audit-trail tapes and quick-look files to be accessed interactively.

 

The greatest power of the database editor lies in its ability to be easily programmed, allowing libraries of user programs (called macros) to be developed for specific or general applications. Using these macros, DBE may scan an entire database in search of some pre-programmed, pre-defined condition (perhaps an abnormality to be located). DBE may also be used to quickly produce reports on database growth, page utilization and even DARP overflow chaining and usage. DBE macros can be used as well to view an audit-trail tape (block by block) to gather ATT statistics or to perform tape verification.

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

| Chapter 1 | A Short History of DBE |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.2. A Short History of DBE

DBE was developed in response to the following collection of needs as identified by database administrators and database system-support personnel:

 

1. There was no way to examine the contents of a DMS/1100 page without using either the DUMP or PRINT commands of the DMU. While these DMU features would allow pages to be inspected, they were far from conversational in nature. Although other more conversational file utilities were available, typically they were not specific enough to be of significant value for use in a DMS/1100 environment.

 

2. Examination of absolute schema elements was possible only using DMR dumps or the @LIST processor.

 

3. No utility existed for automated examination of the physical structures of a database. Of greatest concern was the ability to obtain record-occurrence counts by area, monitor the growth of DARP areas (in an attempt to reduce the number of IO22 conditions on DARP areas) and to monitor the overflow page chaining of areas (to detect any data 'clumping', thus indicating that an area reorganization was needed). While QLP could count record occurrences, counts by area would be difficult to obtain. While the DMU could generate some statistics, time-consuming database verifications or compactions were necessary in order to generate them.

 

4. The available tape utility programs either were too awkward to use interactively or were not specific enough to be used with audit trail tapes.

 

5. The need existed to perform ad hoc scans of areas to locate suspected abnormalities so that they might be corrected. These flaws in the physical structure of the database (broken pointers, corrupted page headers, etc.) were generally of a nature as to make the affected pages unavailable to QLP or the DMU (or even the DMR itself). The DMU was also limited in the set of structures it could access.

 

The database editor was developed to address each of these concerns. In the actual implementation of DBE, a number of design decisions were made that profoundly influenced the character of the final 'product':

 

1. Whenever possible, commands were made interactive in nature; there are very few DBE facilities that can be done only from within a macro and not from the keyboard of the users terminal.

 

2. The command set provided is very powerful. Commands such as the COUNT command allow record occurrence counts to be generated for an area. A SORT statement exists as does a HISTOGRAM statement (to automatically generate histograms for statistical reporting).

 

3. The command syntax was patterned after several commonly-used utility programs; among the utilities contributing to the syntax of DBE commands are @ED, @FANG and @FLIT as well as elements of the programming languages BASIC, COBOL and FORTRAN.

 

4. DBE bypasses the DMR. This decision was made for two basic reasons. First, due to the nature of the problems DBE was designed to solve, it would need to deal with databases that might adversely impact the DMR - not advisable in a multi-thread environment. Second, even though it would have been possible to make DBE a single-thread application for the DMR, it would have been necessary to introduce local code to that DMR in order to accommodate the required DBE accesses. Since much of the DBE activity was intended to be read-only in nature and is performed at times when the DMR is inactive (during a database save or after a DMR failure due to a corrupted database), no serious inconsistencies were anticipated. As a side-effect of this decision not to use the DMR as the database I/O mechanism, DBE can perform many functions at a rate significantly faster than the DMR (with its necessarily generalized code) would be capable of providing.

 

5. DBE was designed to operate as a batch/demand application. While it does allow batch-connect access to TIP schemas and areas, it is not itself available as a TIP transaction program.

 

6. DBE was not intended for use by application programmers, although those familiar with DMS structures might find it useful. DBE was also not intended to interface with DMS/1100 run-units.

 

Originally developed for use in conjunction with DMS/1100 level 7R1, DBE has been enhanced for use with DMS/1100 schemas and subschemas through level 12R4. Since the internal structure of a database has remained consistent across DMS levels, any database should be accessible to DBE provided that a level 7R1 or greater schema absolute can be provided.

1.3. Using DBE to Examine Schemas and Pages

The database editor, as previously mentioned, may be used to 'browse' through schema/subschema elements or database pages. This is accomplished by referring to the appropriate DBE-defined 'tables' using the editor-like printing commands. The tables defined by DBE are shown in Figure 1-1.

 

The DBE Programmer's Reference Manual shows a complete listing of each table.

TREG Table of TIP Registration Information for a TIP area (this is the table returned by the FCSS LF function.

USRBUF Table for use by user macros

 

WBUF The buffer used by the "WRITE" command.

13:PAGE[040]:P,F 012,040

PAGE[012] '@>@]@Q=>@]@5=>@]@>=>@]@*=>]!@#=> 2@#=>@]@ E9HES!D( C-D'

[024] 'D1^B@)A@8AK9A@8AC7BC2A@0A@0AC7A@0A@0AS2A@0AK0A)7A@3AC1A@1D1T'

[036] 'ETHD9^ (NCTC^@)E9H'

14:PAGE[040]:P,A 012,040

PAGE[012] '?H?9$@??$@?5$@?%$@?($BH?$E??$@??WMS-MEDIND8 0829081792000017'

[031] '000032002047031101NYSMOD-SCH WM'

 

Explanation:

 

1. The DBE processor is called. In this example, it is kept in the file 'DMS*UTIL'.

 

2. The SCHEMA command is used to load the absolute schema element (in this case for an EXEC schema) to be used by DBE. The next command, similar to the P! command in @ED, requests a listing of the entire Schema File Description Table. The default format of this listing will be in octal.

 

3. Like @ED, DBE allows the PRINT command to be abbreviated simply to P. A Fieldata listing is now requested. Failure to specify a table name (as is the case here) will cause the most-recently accessed table to be used (note the table name now present in the DBE prompt to the user).

 

4. Print words 0 through 20 (inclusive) of the Schema Reference Table using the default format (octal). Note that the first word of any table is word 0 (zero), not word one.

 

5. Move forward 5 words in the current table from the present word (note the word number within [] in the prompt DBE gives the user).

 

6. Move backward seven words in the current table.

 

7. Print the current word in Fieldata.

 

8. Print words 0 through 20 (inclusive) of the current table (SCRT) in Fieldata.

 

9. Print words 0 through 20 (inclusive) of the current table (SCRT) in decimal.

 

10. Allow DBE to access the given area: areas may be accessed by name (as was the case here), by area code or by position (relative or absolute) within the Schema Reference Table. The AREA command must be executed prior to attempting to reference database pages from that area. Only one area may be in use at any time.

 

11. Fetch page two from the current area and print the entire page header in octal. Only a single page may be in use at any time.

 

12. Print words 10 through 32 (inclusive) of the current page. Note that the word numbers on a PRINT request may be specified in octal (as was done here by supplying a leading zero) or in decimal (as in previous examples).

 

13. Print words 10 through 32 (inclusive) of the current page in Fieldata. No table was specified, so the most recent table (PAGE) is used.

 

14. Print words 10 through 32 (inclusive) of the current page in Ascii.

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

| Chapter 1 | An Introduction to Macros |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.4. Introduction to Macros

Many of the commands and features of the database editor relate to its ability to be programmed for a variety of functions. These programs, called macros, fall into two categories - SYSTEM MACROS, which are supplied with DBE and USER MACROS, written by DBE users to meet their specific needs. There is no difference between these two types of macros other than their locations - system macros are stored as symbolic SDF elements on a file specified when the DBE processor was generated while user macros are stored as symbolic SDF elements on any other file(s) of the user's choosing. DBE will search the files 'DBE$PF' and 'DBE$PF2' for user macros, so the user must attach that name to the appropriate file using @USE or @@USE. The use name for DBE$PF will be attached automatically by DBE by supplying the desired filename in the first parameter subfield (spec1) of the DBE processor call. Additionally, system macros (the LIBRARY, LIB, and ALT-LIB macros) enable the user to switch user macro files at will within DBE.

 

The library of system macros includes the following:

 

1. Macros to assist in the 'browsing' operations described previously. For most tables accessible by DBE (see Figure 1-1), a macro exists to produce a formatted and annotated listing.

 

2. Database access macros to locate and print records, follow set and calc chains and even produce formatted dumps of entire database pages.

 

3. Database reporting macros to assist the database administrator in the task of maintaining a database.

 

4. Macros to perform audit-trail and quick-look file analysis.

 

5. General utility macros to enable the DBE user to converse with the system operator, decode page header date/time stamps and perform other miscellaneous functions.

 

Any macro is invoked by entering its name followed by any necessary parameters either as a command or to the right of an equals sign (=) on a LET (arithmetic assignment) statement. Any macro may be invoked in either fashion. Figure 1-3 illustrates some of the table-printing macros. Note the similarity in form between macro calls and actual DBE commands (only with practice can you tell them apart). By developing macros the effective DBE command-set can easily be extended to include new functionality.

*****************************************

* UTP-SCHEMA *

* *

* START: 12:00:00 *

* TABLE BUILDING COMPLETE: 12:00:01 *

* DIAGRAM COMPLETE: 12:00:02 *

* *

***************************************** end

Explanation:

 

1. The same schema used in Figure 1-2 is loaded for use by DBE. The macro AREAS is then used to list information on all areas defined by that schema.

 

2. The schema is then loaded from tip file 2340 and the AREAS macro is again used. This time however, a mask (*FILE*) is given as a parameter to AREAS - this indicates that only those areas whose names contain the string 'FILE' are to be listed.

 

3. The RECORDS macro is used to list the names of all records in that schema. This macro could have been given a mask for the record names (similar to the mask specified for the AREAS macro).

 

4. The SETS macro is used to produce a listing of the various sets defined in the schema (this could also have been given a mask).

 

5. The FDT macro is used to print a formatted and annotated listing of the file description table. Note how this compares with the FDT listing produced in Figure 1-2.

 

6. The SCRT macro produces a formatted listing of the fixed portion of the Schema Reference Table. The remainder of the SCRT consists of the various area, record, set and feature reference tables - these tables can be listed using other macros or the 'PRINT' command.

 

7. The ART macro produces a listing of the Area Reference Table for the area with code 2343 (area 'FILE-AREA').

 

8. The AREA-DDL macro produces a symbolic schema area entry for the current area. Since the information displayed is obtained solely from the absolute schema tables, some default parameters not existing in the original symbolic schema may be shown by the macro.

 

9. The P0 macro produces a formatted listing of the DAE (page 0) for the current area.

 

10. Schema UTP-SCHEMA is loaded and the DIAGRAM macro is used to produce a schema set diagram. The listing shows the level of the records (the vertical level they would have on a bubble diagram), set relationships and set member record types. Following this listing, an actual bubble diagram is generated.

| Chapter 1 | DMS Database Access Macros |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.5. DMS Database Access Macros

A variety of macros exist to assist in the examination of database pages. These macros may produce formatted listings of the page header, end-of-page slots or even a complete page. Macros also exist to locate and print calc records (using DMSCALC) and follow set or calc chains.

These macros make heavy use of the schema tables in determining such factors about a database as page size, calc chain counts and area type (SARP/DARP).

Figure 1-4 shows two of the simpler macros in this category:

Figure 1-4

Explanation:

 

1. An area is selected for use. Note that, unlike previous examples, the area is selected via its occurrence in the schema; in this case the first area is selected. Since @DDL allocates areas in the schema in ascending order of area code, this selects the area with the lowest area code while '#2' would have selected the second lowest, and so forth.

 

2. Page 1 is loaded. The 'SCHEMA', 'AREA' and 'PAGE' commands, when used with no parameters, will display the values of the schema, area and page currently accessible. Examples of each are shown here.

 

3. All records on the page are 'dumped' using the 'ALLRECS' macro. This will list only the pointer and sort/calc key information for each record on the page; neither the actual data within the record nor the page header/slots will be displayed. The macro 'DUMPREC' is called by the 'ALLRECS' macro to actually produce the listing for each record. The 'ALLRECS' macro will list the records in page-location order. Another macro, 'ALLSLOTS', generates a similar listing in physical slot order.

PTR 6: 450426600013 ==> AREA 01121 (593), PAGE 0266 (182), SLOT 013

SLOT 013: 000005001452

 

RECORD TYPE: 3 (PROB-REC), DBP=450426600013, DBK=450426600005.

AT WORDS 01452-01761, LENGTH: 200. RECORD FLAGS: 050 (CALC, IN USE).

 

SET PROB-RES (OWNER) [01] NEXT: 450426600013 -EMPTY

593/182/11

[02] PRIOR: 450426600013 -EMPTY

593/182/11

SET PROB-STOP (OWNER) [03] NEXT: 450426600013 -EMPTY

593/182/11

SET PROB-SUR (OWNER) [04] NEXT: 450426600013 -EMPTY

593/182/11

[05] PRIOR: 450426600013 -EMPTY

593/182/11

SET AREA-PROB (AUTO MEMBER) [06] NEXT: 450426600012

593/182/10

--CALC POINTERS -------------------------> [07] NEXT: 450426600005

593/182/5

KEY='0034'

Explanation:

 

1. After selecting a schema, a record is located using the DIRECT macro. The area name, page number and record-slot number of the desired record are supplied as parameters on the macro call. A DUMPREC listing is then produced for the record requested. Note that any record may be located in this manner, not just records whose location mode is direct.

 

2. The DIRECT macro is used again, this time supplying only a database key. The key is decoded and the record in record-slot 7 of page 2 in area 702 is displayed.

 

3. After selecting another schema, a calc record within the schema is located. The record's type is that of a 'PROB-REC' and the area in which this record is to be located is 'UTP-PROBLEMS'. Records of this type are defined within the schema as having a four-character calc key. The record desired is that with a key value of '0035'. Using the above information, the DMSCALC macro uses the standard DMS/1100 DMSCALC procedure (an integral part of DBE) to determine that the desired record should be on calc chain 6 of page 182. This chain points to page-slot 12 (the actual record-slot for the record) which in turn points to the record at page word 01762. Examining the record header word, the DMSCALC macro determines that this is indeed a record of the correct type. Now checking the key value, DMSCALC finds the key value to be '0035' (the one we are looking for) and so the record is printed via the DUMPREC macro (called automatically). Had the record not been the one desired, its calc pointer would have been used to locate the next record on the chain and that record would be checked. The process would continue until the record was found or the chain returned to point to the chain header word (a 'no find' condition).

 

4. The DATA macro is used to print the actual data from the record whose header word we are positioned to (the DMSCALC macro positioned us there). Once a record header word has been located, the DATA macro can be used to print its data while the DUMPREC macro may be used to print its pointers.

 

5. Observing that this record is an automatic member of the 'AREA-PROB' set, the decision is made to locate and print the next record in the 'AREA-PROB' set; the listing shows the pointer for this set to be pointer number six (it has been labeled with an arrow for this example in the listing for part 2). The LINK macro is used to link to records given a pointer number within a currently-positioned record. The LINK macro is used to decode pointer #6 (450426600013) and locate/print the record pointed to by that pointer. By repeatedly using the LINK macro we can move from record to record within the set. With an additional option specified on the LINK macro call, this procedure can be automated, displaying records until either a specified number of records have been located or the end of the set has been reached.

In addition to these macros are NEXTREC, which produces a DUMPREC listing of the physical record on the current page; PGSLOT, which produces a DUMPREC listing of a record on the current page given the page slot number of either its calc chain or record slot pointers; and the RECSLOT macro, which will produce a DUMPREC listing of a record given its record-slot number.

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

| Chapter 1 | DMS Database Statistics Macros |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.6. DMS Database Statistics Macros

The macros in this category provide the database administrator with a wide variety of facilities for tracking database growth on both the logical and physical levels.

 

Figure 1-8 shows the use of one macro for determining logical growth of a database.

 

Figure 1-8

Database Statistics Macros

DARP LOGICAL-AREA 08/31/90 14:27-14:27

PRIME : 433 OVERFLOW : 5 (1% OF TOTAL) TOTAL : 438

>10 I

I---------I---------I---------I---------I---------I

0 10 20 30 40 50

 

VERTICAL AXIS SHOWS GROUPS OF POSSIBLE OVERFLOW PAGE COUNTS (1, ... >99)

HORIZONTAL AXIS SHOWS COUNT OF PRIME PAGES FALLING INTO EACH GROUP

DO YOU WISH A DETAILED PAGE DISPLAY (Y/N)?

N

*: end

 

Explanation:

 

1. A schema is loaded and one of its areas selected for use.

 

2. The SCAN-ONE macro is used to produce the following reports:

 

A. SIZE-REPORT information (see the SIZE-REPORT explanation in Figure 1-9). The earliest and latest page update dates (from the database page headers) area also displayed.space

 

B. Record Counts are displayed giving the count of each record type along with the physical record size and name. Note that records that may be placed in an area but have a count of zero are not displayed.

 

C. For Darp areas, a report of page usage to overflow chaining is produced. This report can be useful in identifying areas which may need reorganization due to empty prime pages with chained overflow.

 

D. PAGE-USAGE statistics are produced indicating unused space on Prime and Overflow pages.

 

E. The OVERFLOW report shows counts of prime pages with varying counts of overflow pages chained to them. When run in demand mode, the user is asked if a detailed report is desired. In Batch mode, all primes with > 20 overflow are displayed unless other limits are specified as parameters.

Another method for tracking physical growth would be just monitoring the number of available overflow pages versus the number allocated. As the ratio of the number remaining to be allocated versus the maximum number allocatable decreases, the area grows closer and closer to the infamous 'DARP out' condition where the DMR receives an I/O 22 error attempting to allocate a new overflow page. In addition, SARP areas need to be checked for overflow usage just to see how much is being used so that a re-organization can be done, if necessary. Figure 1-9 illustrates a macro useful in detecting this (and other size-related problems).

 

 

Figure 1-9

Area Sizing Report

fxform 1 *:SCHEMA,T 2340 FMGDEV-SCH 2 *:SIZE-REPORT

 

SIZE REPORT FOR AREAS IN SCHEMA: FMGDEV-SCH 08/31/90 14:28:25 PAGE 1

(A) (B) (C) (D) (E) (F) (G) (H) (I) (J) (K)

-------AREA------ PAGE -----CURRENT USED----- -MAX # PAGES- -O/FLOW GROWTH-

NAME CODE SIZE PRIME OVERFLOW TOTAL FILE SCHEMA MAX. CURR. PCT

============ ==== ==== ====== ========= ====== ====== ====== ===== ===== ===

ATT-AREA-1 2337 1792 101 78-$ 179 199 65535 98 78 20

ATT-AREA-2 2338 1792 101 0 101 149 65535 48 0 100

LOGICAL-AREA 2341 1792 433 21-$ 454 499 4095 66 21 68

LOGICAL-HIST 2342 1792 311 1576-$ 1887 1999 4095 1688 1576 7

FILE-AREA 2343 1792 89 20-$ 109 149 4095 60 20 67

FILE-HIST 2344 1792 267 57 324 399 4095 132 57 57

IO-AREA 2345 1792 499 266 765 999 32767 500 266 47

 

USED TRACKS : 3819 FILE TRACKS : 4393 UNUSED TRACKS : 574 (13%)

 

 

'A-B' IN THE 'OVERFLOW' COLUMN INDICATES CHAINED UNUSED OVERFLOW PAGES

WHERE 'A' IS THE NUMBER OF ALLOCATED OVERFLOW PAGES AND 'B'

IS THE NUMBER OF ALLOCATED, BUT UNUSED, OVERFLOW PAGES

 

'*' AFTER THE PCT FIGURE DENOTES AN AREA WHOSE OVERFLOW GROWTH

HAS FALLEN UNDER 10% end

Explanation:

 

1. A schema is loaded and the SIZE-REPORT macro is executed. this macro allows some measure of pre-detection of the 'DARP-out' condition described earlier. The information printed for each DARP area is as follows (refer to the annotated column headings):

 

(A) Area Name

(B) Area Code

(C) Page Size, in words, as defined within the schema

(D) Number of prime pages allocated to the area, as determined by each area's page zero (DARP area) or schema-specified page count (SARP areas). Any initial-load prime page count expansion (DARP) will be taken into account.

(E) Number of overflow pages currently allocated to the area. This is determined by the areas page zero (DARP) or schema entry (SARP). This count will not include SARP interspersed overflow. SARP areas will be denoted in the report with a '+' immediately to the right of this column. SARP areas having no at-end overflow allocated will be identified as the two in this report were while SARP areas having no at-end overflow IN USE will have a zero here.

(F) Total number of pages in use (D+E).

(G) Maximum area size, in pages, based upon the file assignment (TIP registration or EXEC file size).

(H) Maximum area size, in pages, based upon the number of page bits allowed for database keys by the schema.

(I) Maximum number of overflow pages that could be theoretically be allocated to the area (min(G,H)-D).

(J) Number of overflow pages currently allocated (repeat of E).

(K) Percentage of possible overflow remaining to be allocated (100*(I-J)/I).

Should the value of (K) fall below 10 (or any other value supplied as a parameter to the SIZE-REPORT macro), the area will be flagged with an asterisk (*), as happened here with the FILE-HIST area.

| INTRODUCTION | |

1.7. RDMS Database Access Macros

A variety of macros exist to assist in the examination of RDMS database pages. These macros may produce formatted listings of the page header, end-of-page slots or even a complete page. Many of the macros used for examining DMS areas/pages have RDMS counterparts.

Figure 1-10 shows two of the simpler macros in this category.

 

Figure 1-10

RDMS Page Header/Slots Printing Macros

1 *: RAREA 2399

2 *: RPH 2

 

HEADER FOR PAGE 2 OF RDMS AREA: 2399 (FDT4$)

PREPARED ON 04/07/91 AT 11:47:08

I-----------------------------I [0] 010000000002 I 1 : 2 I FORMAT ID, PAGE #

I-----------------------------I [1] 001600000013 I 896 : 11 I PAGE SIZE, # ACTIVE SLOTS

I-----------------------------I [2] 000756000000 I 494 : 0 I WORDS USED, WORDS DELETED

I-----------------------------I [3] 000743001277 I 483 : 703 I NEXT REC OFFSET, NEXT REC #

I-----------------------------I [4] 000000000000 I I

I-----------------------------I [5] 040513272614 I 040513272614 I CHECKSUM

I-----------------------------I [6] 000000000000 I I [7] 000000000000 I I

I-----------------------------I [010] 000000000146 I 0 : : 102 I DATA PAGE, NEXT PAGE #

I-----------------------------I [011] 000000000000 I 0 : I PAGE DELETED FLAG

I-----------------------------I

3 *:R$SLOTS

**************************************************************************** * RECORD SLOTS * **************************************************************************** 001565 001267000467 SLOT #1 RECORD #695 STARTS AT WORD: 0467 001566 001270000542 SLOT #2 RECORD #696 STARTS AT WORD: 0542 001567 001271000615 SLOT #3 RECORD #697 STARTS AT WORD: 0615 001570 001272000670 SLOT #4 RECORD #698 STARTS AT WORD: 0670 001571 001105000341 SLOT #5 RECORD #581 STARTS AT WORD: 0341 001572 001106000414 SLOT #6 RECORD #582 STARTS AT WORD: 0414 001573 001100000012 SLOT #7 RECORD #576 STARTS AT WORD: 012 001574 001101000065 SLOT #8 RECORD #577 STARTS AT WORD: 065 001575 001102000140 SLOT #9 RECORD #578 STARTS AT WORD: 0140 001576 001103000213 SLOT #10 RECORD #579 STARTS AT WORD: 0213 001577 001104000266 SLOT #11 RECORD #580 STARTS AT WORD: 0266

Explanation:

 

1. An RDMS area is selected for use.

 

2. The RPH macro is used to produce a formatted listing of the page header for page 2 of the area.

 

3. The R$SLOTS macro is used to print the end-of-page slots for the page.

Figure 1-11 shows the use of the R$PGDUMP macro in printing the contents of an entire RDMS database page (this is somewhat similar to the DMS DMU PRINT and DUMP functions).

1. An EXEC RDMS area is selected for use and the first data page is based.

 

2. The RPAGE command is executed to display the current page number and RDMS area name.

 

3. The R$ALLRECS macro is called to dump all records on the page.

 

4. The R$RECSLOT macro is called to dump the record in slot # 1.

 

5. The table pointer is set to word 012 of the current RDMS page (the start of the record from the previous command) and the R$DUMPREC macro is called to dump the record.

 

6. The R$NEXTREC macro is called to dump the next record on the page.

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

| Chapter 1 | Update Capabilities |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.9. Update Capabilities

None of the examples presented thus far have utilized the update capabilities of DBE. The user may modify any table known to DBE, thus changing its in-core value. In addition, the mass-storage copy of the table (schema, page, p0) may be rewritten from the in-core copy. The use of the REWRITE command can be restricted via the DBE security system, if configured. In addition, the REWRITE command can be completely eliminated from DBE via a DBE generation parameter.

No audit trail, rollback or patch indicator features exist for DBE use. The user may manually set the patch indicator on a page however.

Figure 1-15 illustrates how the PATCH macro and REWRITE command might be used (presumably in a dire emergency only) to modify a word on a page. In addition, the DPATCH macro may be used to generate standard DMU PATCH directives when a more controlled facility is desired.

 

Figure 1-15

DBE Update Facilities

1 *:SCH,T 2340 FMGDEV-SCH

*:AREA LOGICAL-AREA

AREA FILE: TIP/DMS FILE: 2341

2 *:PAGE 1

PAGE[0]:LAST PAGE

PAGE[03377] 000000000000

3 PAGE[03377]:PATCH 1

PAGE[03377] 000000000001

4 PAGE[03377]:REWRITE PAGE

5 PAGE[03377]:PAGE,R

PAGE[03377]:LAST PAGE

PAGE[03377] 000000000001

6 PAGE[03377]:DPATCH 0

* PATCH ELEMENT HAS BEEN OPENED - PROCEED *

PATCH AREA LOGICAL-AREA PAGE 1 WORD 1791 REL TO TOP REP

O000000000001 WITH O000000000000.

7 PAGE[03377]:DPATCH,E

* PATCH ELEMENT CREATED SUCCESSFULLY *

8 PAGE[03377]:P

PAGE[03377] 000000000001

9 PAGE[03377]:PRT,S TPF$.PATCHELT

 

DA0*TPF$(0).PATCHELT

 

1: PATCH AREA LOGICAL-AREA PAGE 1 WORD 1791 REL TO TOP REP

2: O000000000001 WITH O000000000000.

 

Explanation:

 

1. A schema is loaded and an area selected for use.

 

2. Page 1 is read and the last word on that page displayed.

 

3. The last word is set to 1. The change is made in core only.

 

4. The page currently in core is written back to the area from which it came.

 

5. The current page is reloaded and its last word is displayed.

 

6. The DPATCH macro is used to change the value of the current page word to zero via the DMU patch facility. The appropriate patch directive is written to the crt and to the patch element (TPF$.PATCHELT). The user may later call the DMU, invoke the appropriate subschema and @add the patch element to actually perform the update.

 

7. This being the only desired change, the patch element is closed. Terminating DBE without first doing a DPATCH,E would automatically close the patch element.

 

8. The current word is reprinted. Notice that it has not actually been modified.

 

9. The DBE PRT macro is used to print the patch element contents.

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

| Chapter 1 | Audit-Trail Capabilities |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.10. Audit Trail Access Capabilities

DBE has the ability to perform generalized I/O to either disc files (on a sector-addressable basis) or tapes (on a block basis). two macros - DUMPATT and ATTPAGE have been developed to allow the user to examine data on a DMS audit trail tape (see the section on UDS support for IRU audit trail tapes). Analysis functions such as run-unit, time/date or area/page searches could easily be developed using the DBE tape access and macro facilities.

Figure 1-16 illustrates these macros at work.

 

Figure 1-16

DBE Audit-Trail Access

CHECKPOINT AT 12/02/81 AT 02:32:00.

 

BLOCK 3:

CHECKPOINT AT 12/02/81 AT 02:50:08.

 

BLOCK 4:

START OF RUN-UNIT WITH AFTER-LOOK FOR AREA 2341, PAGE 1.

WRITTEN BY 'ATTEST' ON 12/02/81 AT 12:52:59.

 

BLOCK 5:

END OF RUN-UNIT.

WRITTEN BY 'ATTEST' ON 12/02/81 AT 12:52:59.

 

BLOCK 6:

START OF RUN-UNIT WITH AFTER-LOOK FOR AREA 319, PAGE 1114.

WRITTEN BY '*51273' ON 12/02/81 AT 12:53:00.

 

BLOCK 7:

| Chapter 1 | UDS Support |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.11. UDS Support

DBE has the ability to access UDS tables in core by reading the various UDS Common Banks. Several macros are avialable to dump the various UDS tables and provide UDS Application related information.

Note that DBE can only READ common banks - no WRITE functionality exists.

Figure 1-17 illustrates some of the UDS access macros.

Figure 1-17

 

FXFORM 1 *:U$APP FMGDEV-SCH

DBE IS CONNECTED TO UDS APPLICATION "APPFOR" (#4) BDI: 0402503

WITH ALIAS FMGDEV-SCH

END

Explanation:

 

1. The U$APP macro was called to determine and base the UDS application for alias name "FMGDEV-SCH".

 

2. The U$ADT macro was called to dump the ADT (Application Description table) from core. The parameter "4" inicated that we only wanted information relating to application number 4. If the parameter was not supplied, all apllications would have been reported on.

 

3. The U$APP-STATUS macro was called to display the status of the current application.

 

4. The U$INDEX macro was called to produce a formatted dump of the DCS index for the current application. This index is used to determine the location and size of many of the UDS tables.

 

5. The U$ACT macro is called to produce a formatted dump of the ACT table for the current application.

 

6. The U$DOWN-LIST macro is called to produce a formatted dump of the DOWN LIST for the current application.

 

7. The U$USE-COUNT macro is called to determine if the specified Schema name is active. This macro is especially useful in schema rollin runstreams. If the schema were active, the macro would wait until the schema was unused.

 

8. The SCHEMATA macro is called with a parameter of "3" to base the schema SCHEMATA for application group 3.

 

9. The U$ALIAS macro is called to display the alias list for each configured application.

 

10. The U$APP macro is once again called to base application group 3. A PRT,S is then requested to display the contents of the APPSGS file which was created by the U$APP macro. This file could then be used as SGS card input to an SSG stream.

 

11. An audit trail tape is assigned and given an @USE name of "ATT". The ATT macro is called to initialize all ATT tables.

 

12. The ATT macro is called to set the blocks to be processed count at 2.

 

13. The ATT macro is called to display all audit records in the next 2 audit blocks.

 

14. The ATT-DUMP macro is called to provide a detailed formatted dump of the current audit record.

 

15. The ATT macro is called with the "D" option to process the next audit record and call the ATT-DUMP macro to format the record.

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

| Chapter 1 | Anatomy of a DBE Macro |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.12. The Anatomy of a DBE Macro

Figure 1-18 presents a basic DBE macro to demonstrate the ease of DBE macro programming. The user macro ALLRUID has already been entered (that process is not shown) by a DBE user. The macro could have been entered directly to DBE (in a manner similar to the creation process of @ED macros) or could be entered (via @ED or @ELT) into an SDF program file element. The manner in which the macro is executed is independent of the manner in which it was defined.

 

This macro will be used to display the altering run-unit id for each prime page within the current area. The DBE 'Macro Trace Mode' is used to trace the execution of the macro.

Figure 1-18

Anatomy of a Macro

1 *:SCHEMA DMS10R1*ABS-FMGDEV.FMGDEV-SCH

*:AREA #1

AREA FILE: TIP/DMS FILE: 2341

fxform 2 *:ART

(BEGIN MACRO 'ALLRUID')

1: PARAMETER LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: LASTPG AT LINE: 1 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* LASTPG: 000000000012

2: IF ART[3]:S6>0 THEN

3: LET P=P0[013]:H2 - 1

4: ELSE

7: IF LASTPG=0 LET LASTPG=P

8: REPEAT I=1,1

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 8 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000001

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 1 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000002

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 2 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000003

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 3 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000004

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 4 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000005

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 5 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000006

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 6 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000007

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 7 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000010

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 8 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000011

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 9 RUN-UNIT ID: DFB100

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

*MONITOR* OF: I AT LINE: 11 OF MACRO: ALLRUID

*MONITOR* I: 000000000012

9: PAGE I

10: WRITE RUID I,PH[4]

------------------------------------

PAGE: 10 RUN-UNIT ID: DA0060

------------------------------------

11: UNTIL I=LASTPG

12: RUID: FORMAT 'PAGE: ',D,' RUN-UNIT ID: ',F6,*

*: RETURN

7 DEBUG:*:DEBUG OFF

 

1. A schema is loaded and its first area selected for use.

 

2. The areas Area Reference Table is displayed. The ALLRUID macro will make use of two ART entries (word 3, S6 and word 010, H2).

 

3. The macro ALLRUID is listed. Note the indentation dynamically produced by DBE at lines 3, 5, 9 and 10. Comments within the macro are preceeded by a '@'. An explanation of the macro code follows.

 

Line 1. A Parameter is accepted on the macro call line to set a limit on the amount of pages to be processed.

 

line 2. The macro checks word 3,,s6 in the ART to see if the DARP indicator is at least 010 (8); this would mean the area is a DARP area.

 

line 3. If this is a DARP area, the variable 'P' is set to the number of pages in the area (prime+overflow). This is calculated by subtracting one from the next available overflow page number, as found in the DAE (page zero).

 

line 5. If this is a SARP area, the variable 'P' is set to the number of allocated pages as found in word 010,,h1 of the area reference table.

 

Line 7. If no limit was set for the amount of pages to be processed, the last prime page (from Line 3 or Line 5 above) will be used.

 

Line 8. A loop is begun, varying the variable I from 1, incrementing by one each time through the loop.

 

line 9. The 'I'th page is loaded into an internal DBE buffer. This will define the DBE table PAGE (database page) and PH (database page header) to the macro.

 

line 10. The page number (I) and the altering run-unit id (stored as six Fieldata characters in word 4 of the page header) are written to the crt using the format RUID. As in FORTRAN, a FORMAT dictates the formatting conventions to be used when printing output lines. In this case, the string 'PAGE: ' will be followed by a variable-length decimal number (the format item 'D') which will be followed by the string 'RUN-UNIT ID:' and will be terminated with 6 Fieldata characters. The presence of the activation code (*) on the format indicates that the buffer, once assembled, is indeed to be printed and, following the print, erased to spaces.

 

Line 11. The loop is bounded by an UNTIL condition that will allow it to continue until 'I' has reached the number of requested pages, 'LASTPG'.

 

4. The macro ALLRUID is invoked with a parameter of '10' being passed and the appropriate output is produced for the current area.

 

5. DBE Debug Mode is turned on and DBE TRACE mode is activated and the ALLRUID macro is again executed. Note how each statement within the macro is printed as it is executed.

 

6. The MONITOR command is used to monitor all alterations made to the variables I and LASTPG. The macro is again executed. Note the output lines beginning with '*MONITOR'; these are the output lines produced by the monitor facility.

 

7. DBE Debug Mode is turned off.

| Chapter 1 | Functions of the |

| INTRODUCTION | Database Editor |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.13. On-line assistance

One of the user-friendly aspects of DBE is its ability to assist the user in the interpretation of errors. Great care has been taken to issue concise yet meaningful error messages to the user (particularly as of level 5R1). No matter how much care is taken, however, users will frequently receive messages which seem, at best, vague. Each error message produced by DBE will be prefixed by an error code in angle-brackets (which may be researched in appendix a of this manual). In addition, the user may take advantage of the EXPLAIN macro, as shown in Figure 1-19.

 

Figure 1-19

The Explain Macro

1 *:SCHEMA SYS$*LIB$.FURPUR

<26> ABSOLUTE NOT SCHEMA/SUBSCHEMA OR HAS WRONG NAME 2 *:EXPLAIN

<26> ABSOLUTE NOT SCHEMA/SUBSCHEMA OR HAS WRONG NAME

After loading the first 28 words of the absolute element requested, DBE has determined that the absolute is either (a) not actually a schema or subschema after all or (b) has determined that the schema/subschema name of what was loaded does not match the name requested.

3 *:EXPLAIN 15

<15> INVALID PRTCN REQUEST

 

The request issued to prtcn$ (through the DBE prtcn command)

is illegal either due to a syntax error within the string/subset

or is too long (greater than 62 words).

 

1. A schema is to be loaded. Obviously what is being loaded is not a schema, so it should be no surprise that DBE issues an error message.

 

2. Not understanding what the error means, the user keys in EXPLAIN to have the most-recently received error explained more completely.

 

3. As shown here, a specific error may be requested as well. Users of cts should find this quite familiar as it was patterned after the cts EXPLAIN command.

Consult the documentation of the EXPLAIN macro for more information on how the explanations are produced.

| Chapter 1 | Using DBE to edit a disk file |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.14. Using DBE to Edit a Disc File

A package of DBE macros exists to allow the user to edit an EXEC or TIP/DMS file on a sector-addressable level (similar to such utilities as @FANG or @FED).

Figure 1-20 illustrates this package at work.

 

Figure 1-20

Using DBE to Edit Disc Files

1 *:DISCEDIT EXEC-8*GARY.

YOU ARE NOW EDITING: EXEC-8*GARY.

WORD/BLOCK NUMBER ORIGIN IS 0

DEFAULT PRINT FORMAT IS OCTAL

BLOCK SIZE IS 28 WORDS 2 [0/0]:GET 0 3 [0/0]:P!,OF

0 octal--------------------------------- Fieldata----------

000000 505025135050 000000020676 000000000000 **pf**@@@]a"@@@@@@

000003 000000000001 001236000000 021312043466 @@@@@[@ey@@@]fe^w6

000006 000000000000 000000000000 021304000000 @@@@@@@@@@@@]f^@@@

000011 000000000000 000000000000 021304000000 @@@@@@@@@@@@]f^@@@

000014 000000000000 000000000000 000000000000 @@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@

000017 000000000000 000000000000 021304060072 @@@@@@@@@@@@]f^a@'

000022 000004000000 000000000061 000000003400 @@^@@@@@@@@1@@@@w@

000025 000000000000 000000000000 000000000000 @@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@

000033* 000221476112 @]l$1e 4 [0/0]:5

0 octal----------------------------------------------------

000005 021312043466 5 [5/5]:PATCH 0,T3

[5/5]:P

0 octal----------------------------------------------------

000005 021312040000 6 [5/5]:EXIT

 

1. The DISCEDIT macro is called to edit the file EXEC-8*GARY. Hereafter, all commands shown are commands to the macro, not to DBE. the default values for all alterable specifications are printed.

 

2. Block zero (since the block size is 28 words, this means sector zero) is read.

 

3. The entire 28-word block just read is printed in octal and Fieldata (ascii is also available via the 'a' option). Note that unlike the DBE PRINT command, multiple print formats may be simultaneously invoked.

 

4. We position to word 5 and it is printed (note that the first word number is word zero).

 

5. Using the PATCH command, the last 12 bits of the current word (t3) are set to zero. the word is printed to verify this.

 

6. The macro is terminated and the user returns to DBE conversational mode.

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

| Chapter 1 | Security Considerations |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.15. Security Considerations

Recognizing the potential for misuse of the database editor, it was decided early on in the development cycle that some form of security enforcement would be required to control its use.

DBE security, which may be excluded via a generation parameter, operates on three levels:

 

1. Batch - Batch usage will be restricted by account number. only batch runs running with a registered account number will be allowed access to DBE.

 

2. Demand - Demand usage will be controlled by userid or account number (a generation parameter selection). Only demand users registered with DBE will be allowed access.

 

3. Siteid - Demand usage can be further controlled by specifying the terminals (by siteid) on which DBE may be accessed.

The security definitions are dynamic - that is they may be established, altered or deleted without the need for a new DBE generation (once security has been configured, of course). The user(s) allowed to modify the DBE security definitions are referred to as 'Master Users'. The userids (or accounts) of each master user may be 'hardcoded' into DBE so that they may still operate even if disaster befalls the DBE security configuration file (whose name, keys, etc. are known only to DBE and at least one master user). In addition a master user may bestow the privilege of being a master user upon any demand user known to DBE. In fact, a master user may grant any user the following privileges:

 

1. Update - Only users (batch or demand) having this privilege may use the REWRITE command. If the DBE generation has been done specifying that patch features are disabled, no user may use the REWRITE command, regardless of privilege assignments. As of level 5R1 this privilege will also control access to the PUT command for sector-addressable disc files (anyone may perform a PUT to an SDF, Non-DMS Tip, or Tape file).

 

2. Ignore-locks - Users with this privilege (batch or demand) need not supply the 'lock for copy' when attempting to use a schema.

 

3. Any-site - Demand users with this privilege may use any terminal, not just those registered with DBE.

 

4. Master - Demand users with this privilege may access and alter the DBE security file.

 

5. Debug - Users with this privilege may access any internal DBE debugging facilities. This is intended for use by those performing DBE development or maintenance duties.

 

6. No-news - Users with this privilege will not be automatically given the DBE system news when they log on to DBE.

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

| Chapter 1 | Logging |

| INTRODUCTION | |

:-------------------------------:--------------------------------:

1.16. Logging

If configured via the DBE sysgen, the following commands will result in system log entries being produced:

 

LOG (used by the PATCH macro)

 

REWRITE

 

PUT to DMS file or Sector Formatted Exec file

Logging can be via an ER LOG$ or ER SYSLOG$ depending on sysgen parameters and operating system level.

1.16.1 LOG$ Logging

If logging is via "ER LOG$", the following log entries will be produced:

 

- An initial entry the first time a log entry is requested. This entry will contain accounting information.

 

- Two entries for each LOG or REWRITE command containing Schema, Subschema and RDMS information. The two entries combined make up one logical entry.

 

- An entry for each PUT to a DMS file or Sector Formatted Exec file. This entry will contain file name / number , start sector and number of words written.

| Chapter 1 | Keyin$ Handler |

1.17. KEYIN$ Handler

If configured via the DBE sysgen, a KEYIN$ handler is automatically enabled at DBE startup. The KEYIN$ handler will allow displaying of current DBE status information.

The KEYIN$ display is activated by a "*<runid>" console keyin, where <runid> is the generated runid of the job executing DBE. Output will be directed to the console or terminal initiating the keyin.

 

Figure 1-23

KEYIN$ Example

 

>@@CONS DA0220

 

DA0220*DBE*SCHEMA: RML-SCH FILE: TIP/DMS FILE 1595

DA0220*DBE*SUBSCH: TRIVIAL FILE: A23456789012*B23456789012.

DA0220*DBE*AREA: 1587 (RML-DARP) FILE: UNI*RML-DARP.

DA0220*DBE*PAGE: 6

DA0220*DBE*RDMS AREA FILE: AUDIT-AP*REELS PAGE: 7

DA0220*DBE*UDS APPLICATION: 4 (APPFOR)

DA0220*DBE*MACRO: LOGBUF STATEMENT #: 15

DA0220*DBE*CMD: WRITE = R
